Elements of Metric Spaces

Elements of Metric Spaces

A function $f$ on $\mathbb{R}$ is continuous at a given point $a ‚àà \mathbb{R}$ iff the image of a point which is close to $a$ is itself close to $f(a)$.

  • The ‚Äúgeometric‚Äù way of thinking about continuity depends intrinsically on the ‚Äúdistance‚Äù between two points.
  • For the continuity of functions defined on more complicated sets, the meaning of the term ‚Äúclose‚Äù is not transparent.
  • Therefore, need the conception of distance between two elements of an arbitrary set

By way of abstraction, the notion of distance function is built only on three properties.

  • These properties are strong enough to introduce to an arbitrary nonempty set a geometry rich enough to build a satisfactory theory of continuous functions.

Definitions

Metric:

  • Let $X$ be any nonempty set. Let $X$ be any nonempty set.
  • A function $d : X √ó X ‚Üí \mathbb{R}_+$ that satisfies the following properties is called a distance function or a metric on $X$:
  • For any $x, y, z ‚àà X$,
    1. (Positive Definite) $d(x,y)=0$ iff $x=y$,
    2. (Symmetry) $d(x, y)=d(y, x)$,
    3. (Triangle Inequality) $d(x, y) ‚â§ d(x, z) + d(z, y)$.

Metric Space:

  • If $d$ is a distance function on $X$, we say that $(X, d)$ is a metric space, and refer to the elements of $X$ as points in $(X, d)$.

Metric Subspace:

  • If $(X,d)$ is a metric space and $‚àÖ\neq Y ‚äÇX$.
  • $(Y, d|_{Y √óY} )$, or simply $Y$, is a metric subspace of $X$
  • $d$ is called the inherited metric.

Sequence:

  • A sequence in a nonempty set $X$ is a function $f : \mathbb{N }‚Üí X$,
  • This function is represented as $(x_1,x_2,‚Ä¶)$ where $x_i := f(i)$ for each $i ‚àà \mathbb{N}$.
  • The set of all sequences in $X$ is $X^{\mathbb{N}}$ or $X^‚àû$.

Subsequence:

  • A subsequence of a sequence $f ‚àà X^\mathbb{N}$ is a function of the form $f ‚ó¶œÉ$ where $œÉ :\mathbb{N} ‚Üí \mathbb{N}$ is strictly increasing
  • That is, $œÉ(k) < œÉ(l)$ for any $k,l ‚àà N$ with $k < l$.
  • This function is represented as the array $(x_{m_1},x_{m_2},‚Ä¶)$ with $m_k = œÉ(k)$ and $x_{m_k} = f(m_k)$ for each $k = 1,2,‚Ä¶.$

Increasing, Decreasing, Monotonic:

  • A real sequence $(x_m)$ is said to be increasing if $x_m ‚â§ x_{m+1}$ for each $m ‚àà\mathbb{N}$, and strictly increasing if $x_m < x_{m+1}$ for each $m ‚àà\mathbb{N}$.
  • It is said to be (strictly) decreasing if $(‚àíx_m)$ is (strictly) increasing.
  • A real sequence which is either increasing or decreasing is referred to as a monotonic sequence.

Convergent Sequence:

  • A sequence $(a_n)_{n\in\mathbb{N}}$ of points is a convergent sequence in a metric spaces $X$ if, given any real number $\varepsilon > 0$, there exists an integer $N_\varepsilon\in\mathbb{N}$ such that if $n \geq N_\varepsilon$, then $d(a_n, L) < \varepsilon$.

  • $L$ is called the sequitial limit.

Cauchy Sequence:

  • A sequence $(a_n)_{n\in\mathbb{N}}$ of real numbers is a Cauchy sequence in a metric spaces $X$ if, given any real number $\varepsilon > 0$, there exists an integer $N_\varepsilon\in\mathbb{N}$ such that if $n, m \geq N_\varepsilon$, then $d(a_n, a_m) < \varepsilon$.

Intuitively speaking, by a Cauchy sequence we mean a sequence the terms of which eventually get arbitrarily close to one another.

Subsequential Limit:

  • Given any $(x_m) ‚àà \mathbb{R}^‚àû$, we say that $x ‚àà \overline{\mathbb{R}} $ is a subsequential limit of $(x_m)$ if there exists a subsequence $(x_{m_k} )$ with $x_{m_k} ‚Üí x$ as $k ‚Üí ‚àû$
  • That is, if, for any $Œµ>0$, there exists a $K‚àà\mathbb{N} $ such that $¬†|x_m ‚àíx|<Œµ$ for all $k‚â•K$.

Limit Supremum, Limit Infimum:

  • Let $(a_k)_{k‚àà\mathbb{N}}$ be a bounded sequence of real numbers.
  • For each $n ‚àà \mathbb{N}$, define $b_n = \sup{a_k | k ‚â• n}$, and $c_n = \inf{a_k | k ‚â• n}$.
  • We define the limit supremum of the sequence $(a_k){k‚àà\mathbb{N}}$ to be $\lim{n‚Üí‚àû} b_n$, and we denote this by $\lim \sup_{k‚Üí‚àû} a_k$.
  • We define the limit infimum of $(a_k){k‚àà\mathbb{N}}$ similarly: $\lim \inf{k‚Üí‚àû} a_k = \lim_{n‚Üí‚àû} c_n$.

Propositions and Theorems

Young‚Äôs Inequality:

  • Suppose $a,b\geq 0$ in $\mathbb{R}$.

  • Suppose $p,q\in \mathbb{R}$ with $p,q>1$ such that $\frac{1}{p}+\frac{1}{q} = 1$. Then
    $$
    ab\leq\frac{a^p}{p}+\frac{b^q}{q}
    $$

Theorem 3.2.7: (H√∂lder‚Äôs Inequality)

  • Suppose $x = (x_1,x_2,‚Ä¶,x_n) ‚àà \mathbb{R}^n$ and $y = (y_1,y_2,‚Ä¶,y_n) ‚àà \mathbb{R}^n$

  • Suppose $p,q\in \mathbb{R}$ with $p,q>1$ such that $\frac{1}{p}+\frac{1}{q} = 1$. Then
    $$
    \sum^n_{k=1}|x_k-y_k|\leq(\sum^n_{k=1}|x_k|^p)^\frac{1}{p}(\sum^n_{k=1}|y_k|^q)^\frac{1}{q}
    $$

Minkowski‚Äôs Inequality 1:

  • For any $n ‚àà \mathbb{R} $, $a_i,b_i ‚àà\mathbb{R}, i = 1,‚Ä¶,n$, and any $1 ‚â§ p <‚àû$,

$$
\left(\sum^n_{i=1}|a_i +b_i|^p \right)^{\frac1{p}}\leq\left(\sum^n_{i=1}|a_i |^p \right)^{\frac1{p}}+\left(\sum^n_{i=1}|b_i|^p \right)^{\frac1{p}}
$$

Minkowski‚Äôs Inequality 2:

  • For any $(x_m),(y_m) ‚àà \mathbb{R}^\infty $, and $1\leq p<\infty $,

$$
\left(\sum^n_{i=1}|x_i +y_i|^p \right)^{\frac1{p}}\leq\left(\sum^n_{i=1}|x_i |^p \right)^{\frac1{p}}+\left(\sum^n_{i=1}|y_i|^p \right)^{\frac1{p}}
$$

Examples and Exercises

Discrete Metric Space:

  • Let $X$ be any nonempty set and, for $x_1,x_2 ‚àà X$, define
    $$
    d(x_1,x_2)=\left\{\begin{matrix}0&if\ x_1=x_2\\ 1& if\ x_1\neq x_2\end{matrix}\right.
    $$

  • This is called the discrete metric, the pair $(X,d)$ is referred to as a discrete metric space.

It is designed to disabuse people of the notion that every metric looks like the usual metric on $\mathbb{R}^n$.

The discrete metric is very handy for producing counterexamples.

$(\mathbb{R}^n, d_p) =\mathbb{R}^{n,p}=\ell^p_n(\mathbb{R})$

  • For each $1‚â§p‚â§‚àû$, where $d_p :\mathbb{R}^n√ó\mathbb{R}^n ‚Üí\mathbb{R}_+$ is defined by

    $$
    d_p(x, y) := \|x-y\|_p =\left(\sum^n_{i=1}|x_i‚àíy_i|^p\right)^{\frac{1}{p}}\ \mbox{for}\ \ 1 ‚â§ p < ‚àû,
    $$

  • and

$$
d_p(x,y):=\max{|x_i ‚àíy_i|:i=1,‚Ä¶,n}\ \ \ \ \mbox{for}\ \ p=‚àû.
$$

$(\mathbb{R}^n, d_p)$ is not a metric space for $p < 1$.

$(\ell^p, d_p)$:

  • For any $1 ‚â§ p < ‚àû$, we define
    $$
    \ell^p :={(x_m)‚àà\mathbb{R}^‚àû : \sum^‚àû_{i=1} |x_i|^p <‚àû}.
    $$

  • and $d_p : \ell^p √ó \ell^p ‚Üí \mathbb{R} _+$ is defined by
    $$
    d_p ((x_m) , (y_m)) := \left(\sum^\infty_{i=1}|x_i ‚àí y_i|^p\right)^\frac1{p} .
    $$

Any $\ell^p$ space is smaller than the set of all real sequences $\mathbb{R}^‚àû$ since the members of such a space are real sequences that are either bounded or that satisfy some form of a summability condition (that ensures that $d_p$ is real-valued).

Indeed, no $d_p$ defines a distance function on the entire $\mathbb{R}^‚àû$.

$(\ell^\infty, d_\infty)$:

  • $\ell^‚àû$ is the set of all bounded real sequences,
    $$
    \ell^‚àû :={(x_m)‚àà\mathbb{R}^‚àû :\sup{|x_m|:m‚àà\mathbb{N}}<‚àû}.
    $$

  • and $d_‚àû : \ell^‚àû √ó\ell^‚àû ‚Üí \mathbb{R} _+$ is defined by

$$
d_‚àû ((x_m) , (y_m)) := |x_m ‚àí y_m|_‚àû=\sup{|x_m ‚àí y_m| : m ‚àà \mathbb{N}}.
$$

  • This metric is called the sup-metric on the set of all bounded real sequences.

$(\mathbf{B}(T), d_\infty)$:

  • Let $T$ be any nonempty set.

  • $\mathbf{B}(T)$ is the set of all bounded real functions defined on $T$,
    $$
    \mathbf{B}(T):={f ‚àà\mathbb{R}^T :\sup{|f(x)|:x‚ààT}<‚àû}.
    $$

  • and $d_‚àû : \mathbf{B}(T) √ó\mathbf{B}(T ) ‚Üí \mathbb{R}_+$ is defined by
    $$
    d_‚àû (f, g) := |f ‚àí g|_‚àû=\sup{|f (x) ‚àí g(x)| : x ‚àà T }.
    $$

An $n$-vector or a sequence can always be thought of as special functions

$\mathbf{B}({1, ‚Ä¶, n})$ coincides with $\mathbb{R}^{n,‚àû}$ (for any $n ‚àà \mathbb{N}$)

$\mathbf{B}(\mathbb{N})$ coincides with $\ell^‚àû$.

Every continuous function on $[a, b]$ is bounded, and hence $\mathbf{C}[a, b] ‚äÜ\mathbf{B}[a, b]$.

Consequently, we can consider $\mathbf{C}[a, b]$ as a metric subspace of $\mathbf{B}[a, b]$.

Countability

Countability

Definitions

Countably Infinite, Countable, Uncountable:

  • A set $X$ is called countably infinite if there exists a bijection $f$ that maps $X$ onto the set $\mathbb{N} $ of natural numbers.
  • $X$ is called countable if it is either finite or countably infinite.
  • $X$ is called uncountable if it is not countable.

Propositions and Theorems

Proposition 1:

  • Every subset of a countable set is countable.

Proposition 2:

  • A countable union of countable sets is countable.

Corollary 1: (Cantor)

  • $\mathbb{Q} $ is countable.

Proposition 4: (Cantor)

  • $\mathbb{R} $ is uncountable.

Elements of Set Theory

Elements of Set Theory

Definitions

Subset, Proper Subset:

  • If every member of $A$ is also a member of $B$, then $A$ is a subset of $B$ and write $A‚äÜB$.

    • $ A=B$ holds iff both $A‚äÜB$ and $B‚äÜA$ hold.

  • If $A‚äÜB$ but $A\neq B$, then $A$ is a proper subset of $B$, and write $A ‚äÇ B$„ÄÇ

Finite, Cardinality, Singleton, Infite:

  • For any set $S$ that contains finitely many elements (in which case we say $S$ is finite), we denote by $|S|$ the total number of elements that $ S$ contains, and refer to this number as the cardinality of $S$.
    • $S$ is a singleton if $|S| = 1$.
  • If $S$ contains infinitely many elements (in which case we say $S$ is infinite), then we write $|S| = ‚àû$.

Empty Set:

  • The set that contains no elements is defined as an empty set, that is,
    $$
    ‚àÖ := {x : x \neq x},
    $$

    • We have $‚àÖ ‚äÜ S$ for any set $S$, it implies that $‚àÖ$ is unique.
    • If $S \neq ‚àÖ$, we say that $S$ is nonempty.
    • ${‚àÖ}$ is a nonempty set.

Power Set:

  • We define the class of all subsets of a given set $S$ as

$$
2^S :={T :T ‚äÜS},
$$

  • which is called the power set of $S$.

Union, Intersection, Difference:

  • Given any two sets $A$ and $B$,
  • The union of $A$ and $B$, denoted as $A ‚à™ B$, is defined as the set ${x : x ‚àà A\ \mbox{or} \ x ‚àà B}$.
  • The intersection of $A$ and $B$, denoted as $A ‚à© B$, is defined as the set ${x : x ‚àà A\ \mbox{and}\ x ‚àà B}$.
    • If $A ‚à© B = ‚àÖ$, we say that $A$ and $B$ are disjoint.
    • If $A ‚äÜ B$, then $A‚à™B = B$ and $A‚à©B = A$.
    • $‚àÖ ‚à™ S = S$ and $‚àÖ ‚à© S = ‚àÖ$ for any set $S$.
  • The difference between $A$ and $B$, denoted as $A\setminus B$, is defined as the set ${ x : x ‚àà A\ \mbox{and}\ x ‚àà/ B } $.

Class, Family, Member:

  • ‚ÄúClass‚Äù or ‚Äúfamily‚Äù refer to a nonempty collection of sets.

  • If $\mathcal{A}$ is a class, then $\mathcal{A} \neq ‚àÖ$ and any member $A ‚àà \mathcal{A}$ is a set.

  • The union of all members of this class, $\bigcup \mathcal{A}$, $\bigcup_{A‚àà\mathcal{A}}A$, is defined as the set ${x : x ‚àà A\ \mbox{for some}\ A ‚àà \mathcal{A}}$.

    • To specify a class $\mathcal{A}$, we can designate a set $I$ as a set of indices
    • If $I={k,k+1,\dots, K}$ for some integers $k$ and $K$ with $k<K$, then we often write $\bigcup^K A_i$, $\bigcup_{i=k}^KA_i$ for $\bigcup_{i\in I}A_i$.
    • If $I={k,k+1,\dots,}$ for some integer $k$, then we may write $\bigcup^\infty A_i$, $\bigcup_{i=k}^\infty A_i$ for $\bigcup_{i\in I}A_i$

  • The intersection of all sets in $\mathcal{A}$, $\bigcap \mathcal{A}$ , $\bigcap_{A‚àà\mathcal{A}}A$, is defined as the set ${x : x ‚àà A\ \mbox{for each}\ A ‚àà \mathcal{A}}$.

Ordered Pair:

  • An ordered pair is an ordered list $(a,b)$ consisting of two objects $a$ and $ b$.

(Cartesian) Product:

  • The (Cartesian) product of $n$ sets $A_1, ‚Ä¶, A_n$, is then defined as

$$
A_1 √ó¬∑¬∑¬∑√óA_n :={(a_1,‚Ä¶,a_n):a_i‚ààA_i, i=1,‚Ä¶,n}.
$$

(Binary) Relation:

  • Let $X$ and $Y$ be two nonempty sets.

  • A subset $R$ of $X √ó Y$ is called a (binary) relation from $X$ to $Y$.

Reflexive, Transitive, Symmetric, Antisymmetric,¬†Complete:

  • A relation $R$ on a nonempty set $X$ is said to be
    • reflexive if $xRx$ for each $x ‚àà X$
    • transitive if $xRy$ and $yRz$ imply $xRz$ for any $x,y,z ‚àà X.$
    • symmetric if, for any $x, y ‚àà X$, $xRy$ implies $yRx$,
    • antisymmetric if, for any $x, y ‚àà X$, $xRy$ and $yRx$ imply $x = y$.
    • complete if either $xRy$ or $yRx$ holds for each $x,y ‚àà X$.

Equivalence Relation, Equivalence Class:

  • A relation $‚àº$ on a nonempty set $X$ is called an equivalence relation if it is reflexive, symmetric and transitive.

  • For any $x ‚àà X$, the equivalence class of $x$ relative to $‚àº$ is defined as the set
    $$
    [x]_‚àº :={y‚ààX:y‚àºx}.
    $$

  • Let $X$ be a set.

  • An equivalence relation on $X$ is a relation $R$ on $X$ such that

    1. (Reflexive) For all $x ‚àà X$, $x ‚àº x$.
    2. (Symmetric) For $x,y‚ààX$, if $x‚àºy$ then $y‚àºx$.
    3. (Transitive) For $s,y,z‚ààX$, if $x‚àºy$ and $y‚àºz$, then $x‚àºz$.

Preorder, Partial order, Linear order:

  • A relation $\succsim$ on a nonempty set $X$ is called a **preorder **on $X$ if it is transitive and reflexive.

  • $\succsim$ is said to be a partial order on $X$ if it is an antisymmetric preorder on $X$.

  • $\succsim$ is called a linear order on $X$ if it is a partial order on $X$ which is complete.

  • By a preordered set we mean a list $(X, \succsim)$ where $X$ is a nonempty set and $\succsim$ is a preorder on $X$.

  • If $\succsim$ is a partial order on $X$, then $(X, \succsim)$ is called a poset (short for partially ordered set)

  • If $\succsim$ is a linear order on $X$, then $(X, \succsim)$ is called either a chain or a loset (short for linearly ordered set).

Function / Map:

  • By a function / map $f$ that maps $X$ into $Y$, denoted as $f:X‚ÜíY$, we mean a relation $f‚ààX√óY$ such that

    1. For every $x‚ààX$, there exists a $y‚ààY$ such that $xfy$,
    2. For every $y,z‚ààY$ with $xfy$ and $xfz$, we have $y=z$.

  • $X$ is the domain of $f$ and $Y$ the codomain of $f$.

  • The range of $f$ is
    $$
    f(X):={y‚ààY :xfy\ \mbox{for some}\ x‚ààX}.
    $$

  • The set of all functions that map $X$ into $Y$ is denoted by $Y^X$ or $f : X ‚Üí Y$ .

  • When $ f(x) = y$, we refer to $y$ as the image or value of $x$ under $f$.

Composition:

  • Given functions $f : X ‚Üí Z$ and $g : Z ‚Üí Y$,

  • The composition of $f$ and $g$ is the function $g‚ó¶f : X ‚Üí Y$, where $g‚ó¶f(x) := g(f(x))$.

  • $(g ‚ó¶ f )(x)$ is usually written as $g ‚ó¶ f (x$).

Surjection, Injection, Bijection:

  • If its range equals its codomain, that is, if $f(X) = Y$, then one says that $f$ maps $X$ onto $Y$, and refers to it as a surjection.
  • If $f$ maps distinct points in its domain to distinct points in its codomain, that is, if $x\neq y$ implies $f(x) \neq f(y)$ for all $x,y ‚àà X$, then we say that $f$ is an injection.
  • If $f$ is both injective and surjective, then it is called a bijection.

Self-map, Identity Function:

  • A function whose domain and codomain are identical, that is, a function in $X^X$, is called a self-map on $X$.
  • The identity function is a self-map on $X$, denoted as $\mbox{id}_X$, and defined as $\mbox{id}_X (x) := x$ for all $x ‚àà X$.

$\mbox{id}_X$ is a bijection.

Binary Operation:

  • Let $X$ be any nonempty set.
  • We refer to a function of the form $‚Ä¢ : X √óX ‚Üí X$ as a binary operation on $X$, and write $x‚Ä¢y$ instead of $‚Ä¢(x,y)$ for any $x, y ‚àà X$.

Field:

  • Let $X$ be any nonempty set, let $+$ and $¬∑$ be two binary operations on $X$.

  • The list $(X, +, ¬∑)$ is called a field if the following properties are satisfied:

    1. (Closure) $x+y\in X$ and $xy\in X$ for all $x,y‚ààX$;
    2. (Commutativity) $x+y=y+x$ and $xy=yx$ for all $x,y‚ààX$;
    3. (Associativity) $(x+y)+z = x+(y+z)$ and $(xy)z = x(yz)$ for all $x,y,z ‚àà X$;
    4. (Existence of Identity Elements) There exist elements $0$ and $1$ in $X$ such that $0 + x = x = x + 0$ and $1x = x = x1$ for all $x ‚àà X$;
    5. (Existence of Inverse Elements)
      • (the additive inverse of $x$) For each $x ‚àà X$, there exists an element $‚àíx$ in $X$ such that $x+‚àíx = 0 = ‚àíx+x$, and
      • (the multiplicative inverse of $x$) For each $x ‚àà X\backslash{0}$, there exists an element $x^{‚àí1}$ in $X$ such that $xx^{‚àí1} = 1 = x^{‚àí1}x$.
    6. (Distributivity) $x(y + z) = xy + xz$ for all $x, y, z ‚àà X$;
    7. (Cancellation) If $xz=yz$ and $z\neq0$ then $x=y$ for all $x,y,z ‚àà X$;

  • A field without the multiplicative inverse of $x$ and cancellation rule for the multiplication is a commutative ring with 1.

Ordered Field:

  • The list $(X,+,¬∑,‚â•)$ is called an ordered field if $(X,+,¬∑)$ is a field, and if $‚â•$ is a partial order on $X$ such that
    1. (Addition) If $x,y,z\in X$, and $x\leq y$, then $x+z\leq y+z$
    2. (Multiplication by nonnegative elements) If $x,y,z\in X$, and $x\leq y,\ 0\leq z$ then $xz\leq yz$
  • The list $(X,+,¬∑,<)$ is called an **ordered field** if $(X,+,¬∑)$ is a field, and if $<$ is a relation on $X$ such that
    1. (Trichotomy) If $x,y\in X$, then $x<y,\ x=y$ or $x>y$
    2. (Transitivity) If $x,y\in X$, and $x<y,\ y<z$, then $x<z$
    3. (Addition) If $x,y,z\in X$, and $x<y$, then $x+z<y+z$
    4. (Multiplication by postive elements) If $x,y,z\in X$, and $x<y,\ 0<z$ then $xz<yz$
  • We adopt the following notation:

$$
X_+ :={x‚ààX :x‚â•0}\ \mbox{and} \ X_{++} :={x‚ààX :x>0},
$$

$$
X_‚àí :={x‚ààX :x‚â§0}\ \mbox{and} \ X_{‚àí‚àí} :={x‚ààX :x<0}.
$$

Absolute Value, The Triangle Inequality:

  • Let $(X, +, ¬∑, ‚â•)$ be an ordered field.
  • The function $|¬∑| : X ‚Üí X$ defined by

$$
|x|:=\left\{\begin{matrix}x,if\ x\geq0\\-x,\ if\ x <0\end{matrix}\right.
$$

  • is called the absolute value function.
  • The following is called the triangle inequality:

$$
|x+y|‚â§|x|+|y|\ \ \ for\ all\ x,y‚ààX.
$$

  • It is valid within any ordered field.

Propositions and Theorems

Proposition 2:

  • Let $X$ and $Y$ be two nonempty sets. A function $f ‚àà Y^X$ is invertible if, and only if, it is a bijection.

Proposition 3:

  • Let $X$ and $Y$ be two nonempty sets. A function $f ‚àà Y^X$ is invertible if, and only if, there exists a function $g‚ààX^Y$ such that $g‚ó¶f=\mbox{id}_X$ and $f‚ó¶g=\mbox{id}_Y$.

Proposition 6:

  • (The Archimedean Property) For any $(a, b) ‚àà \mathbb{R}_{++} √ó \mathbb{R}$, there exists an $m ‚àà\mathbb{N}$ such that $b < ma$.
  • For any $a, b ‚àà \mathbb{R}$ such that $a < b$, there exists a $q ‚àà\mathbb{Q}$ such that $a < q < b$.
